</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below plainlist" style="border-top:1px solid #aaa;line-height:1.6em;font-weight:bold;padding-bottom:0.4em;border-bottom:1px solid #aaa;">
<div class="hlist hlist-separated">
<ul><li><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="185" data-file-width="180" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/16px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/23px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/31px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png 2x" title="Category" width="16"/> <a href="/wiki/Category:Greek_letters" title="Category:Greek letters">Category</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Greek_alphabet_sidebar" title="Template:Greek alphabet sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Greek_alphabet_sidebar" title="Template talk:Greek alphabet sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Greek_alphabet_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Greek alphabet</b> has been used to write the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek language</a> since the late 9th or early 8th century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECook19879_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECook19879-4">[4]</a></sup> It is derived from the earlier <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">Phoenician alphabet</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> and was the earliest known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alphabetic_script" title="Alphabetic script">alphabetic script</a> to have distinct letters for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vowels" title="Vowels">vowels</a> as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consonants" title="Consonants">consonants</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greece" title="Archaic Greece">Archaic</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical</a> times, the Greek alphabet existed in <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greek_alphabets" title="Archaic Greek alphabets">many local variants</a>, but, by the end of the 4th century BCE, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euclidean_alphabet" title="Euclidean alphabet">Euclidean alphabet</a>, with 24 letters, ordered from <a href="/wiki/Alpha" title="Alpha">alpha</a> to <a href="/wiki/Omega" title="Omega">omega</a>, had become standard and it is this version that is still used for Greek writing today.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Letter_case" title="Letter case">uppercase and lowercase forms</a> of the 24 letters are: 
</p>
<dl><dd><span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%91" title="Α">Α</a> α</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%92" title="Β">Β</a> β</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%93" title="Γ">Γ</a> γ</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%94" title="Δ">Δ</a> δ</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%95" title="Ε">Ε</a> ε</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%96" title="Ζ">Ζ</a> ζ</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%97" title="Η">Η</a> η</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%98" title="Θ">Θ</a> θ</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%99" title="Ι">Ι</a> ι</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9A" title="Κ">Κ</a> κ</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9B" title="Λ">Λ</a> λ</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9C" title="Μ">Μ</a> μ</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9D" title="Ν">Ν</a> ν</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9E" title="Ξ">Ξ</a> ξ</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9F" title="Ο">Ο</a> ο</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a href="/wiki/Pi_(letter)" title="Pi (letter)">Π</a> π</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%A1" title="Ρ">Ρ</a> ρ</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%A3" title="Σ">Σ</a> σ</span></span>/ς, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%A4" title="Τ">Τ</a> τ</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%A5" title="Υ">Υ</a> υ</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%A6" title="Φ">Φ</a> φ</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%A7" title="Χ">Χ</a> χ</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%A8" title="Ψ">Ψ</a> ψ</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%A9" title="Ω">Ω</a> ω</span></span>.</dd></dl>
<p>The Greek alphabet is the ancestor of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">Cyrillic scripts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulmas1996_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulmas1996-6">[6]</a></sup> Like Latin and Cyrillic, Greek originally had only a single form of each letter; it developed the <a href="/wiki/Letter_case" title="Letter case">letter case</a> distinction between uppercase and lowercase in parallel with Latin during the <a href="/wiki/Modern_era" title="Modern era">modern era</a>. Sound values and conventional transcriptions for some of the letters differ between <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">Modern Greek</a> usage because the pronunciation of Greek has changed significantly between the 5th century BCE and today. Modern and Ancient Greek also use different <a href="/wiki/Greek_diacritics" title="Greek diacritics">diacritics</a>, with modern Greek keeping only the <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">stress</a> accent (<a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">acute</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Diaeresis_(diacritic)" title="Diaeresis (diacritic)">diaeresis</a>.
</p><p>Apart from its use in writing the Greek language, in both its ancient and its modern forms, the Greek alphabet today also serves as a source of <a href="/wiki/Greek_letters_used_in_mathematics,_science,_and_engineering" title="Greek letters used in mathematics, science, and engineering">technical symbols and labels</a> in many domains of mathematics, science, and other fields.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Letters">Letters</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sound_values">Sound values</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Greek_orthography" title="Greek orthography">Greek orthography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pronunciation_of_Ancient_Greek_in_teaching" title="Pronunciation of Ancient Greek in teaching">Pronunciation of Ancient Greek in teaching</a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manners_of_articulation" title="Manners of articulation">Manners of articulation</a></div>
<p>In both Ancient and Modern Greek, the letters of the Greek alphabet have fairly stable and consistent symbol-to-sound mappings, making pronunciation of words largely predictable. Ancient Greek spelling was generally near-<a href="/wiki/Phonemic_orthography" title="Phonemic orthography">phonemic</a>. For a number of letters, sound values differ considerably between Ancient and Modern Greek, because their pronunciation has followed a set of systematic phonological shifts that affected the language in its post-classical stages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horrocks_231_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-horrocks_231-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<table cellpadding="10" cellspacing="0" style="text-align:center;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="vertical-align:top;">

<td>Short: u as in French <i>l<u>u</u>ne</i><br/>Long: u as in French <i>r<u>u</u>se</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mastronarde2013p12_27-11"><a href="#cite_note-Mastronarde2013p12-27">[18]</a></sup></td>

<td>i as in English <i>mach<u>i</u>ne</i>, but short
</td></tr>

<td>p as in English <i><u>p</u>ot</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mastronarde2013p13_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mastronarde2013p13-32">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thetaphichi_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-thetaphichi-22">[note 2]</a></sup></td>

<dl><dt>Notes</dt></dl>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-zetashift-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-zetashift_20-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">By around 350 BC, zeta in the Attic dialect had shifted to become a single fricative, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_fricative" title="Voiced alveolar fricative">z</a>]</span>, as in modern Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mastronarde2013p11_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mastronarde2013p11-19">[16]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-thetaphichi-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-thetaphichi_22-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-thetaphichi_22-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-thetaphichi_22-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The letters <a href="/wiki/Theta" title="Theta">theta</a> ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">θ</span></span>⟩, <a href="/wiki/Phi" title="Phi">phi</a> ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">φ</span></span>⟩, and <a href="/wiki/Chi_(letter)" title="Chi (letter)">chi</a> ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">χ</span></span>⟩ are normally taught to English speakers with their modern Greek pronunciations of <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_fricative" title="Voiceless dental fricative">θ</a>]</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_labiodental_fricative" title="Voiceless labiodental fricative">f</a>]</span>, and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_fricative" title="Voiceless velar fricative">x</a>]</span> ~ <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_palatal_fricative" title="Voiceless palatal fricative">ç</a>]</span> respectively, because these sounds are easier for English speakers to distinguish from the sounds made by the letters <a href="/wiki/Tau" title="Tau">tau</a> (<span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_plosive" title="Voiceless alveolar plosive">t</a>]</span>), <a href="/wiki/Pi_(letter)" title="Pi (letter)">pi</a> (<span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_bilabial_plosive" title="Voiceless bilabial plosive">p</a>]</span>), and <a href="/wiki/Kappa" title="Kappa">kappa</a> (<span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_plosive" title="Voiceless velar plosive">k</a>]</span>) respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mastronarde2013pp1113_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mastronarde2013pp1113-21">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KellerRussell2012pp56_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-KellerRussell2012pp56-18">[15]</a></sup> These are not the sounds they made in classical Attic Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mastronarde2013pp1113_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mastronarde2013pp1113-21">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KellerRussell2012pp56_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-KellerRussell2012pp56-18">[15]</a></sup> In classical Attic Greek, these three letters were always <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspirated_consonants" title="Aspirated consonants">aspirated consonants</a>, pronounced exactly like tau, pi, and kappa respectively, only with a blast of air following the actual consonant sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mastronarde2013pp1113_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mastronarde2013pp1113-21">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KellerRussell2012pp56_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-KellerRussell2012pp56-18">[15]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-lambdaorlabda-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-lambdaorlabda_26-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although the letter <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">Λ</span></span> is almost universally known today as <i>lambda</i> (<span lang="el"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%BB%CE%AC%CE%BC%CE%B2%CE%B4%CE%B1#Greek" title="wikt:λάμβδα">λάμβδα</a></span>), the most common name for it during the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Greek Classical Period</a> (510–323 BC) appears to have been <i>labda</i> (<span lang="el"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%BB%CE%AC%CE%B2%CE%B4%CE%B1#Greek" title="wikt:λάβδα">λάβδα</a></span>), without the <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">μ</span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KellerRussell2012p5_11-10"><a href="#cite_note-KellerRussell2012p5-11">[11]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-nicholas_finalsigma-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-nicholas_finalsigma_31-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The letter <a href="/wiki/Sigma" title="Sigma">sigma</a> ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">Σ</span></span>⟩ has two different lowercase forms in its standard variant, ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">σ</span></span>⟩ and ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ς</span></span>⟩, with ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ς</span></span>⟩ being used in word-final position and ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">σ</span></span>⟩ elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KellerRussell2012pp56_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-KellerRussell2012pp56-18">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mastronarde2013p12_27-6"><a href="#cite_note-Mastronarde2013p12-27">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nick2004_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nick2004-28">[19]</a></sup> In some 19th-century typesetting, ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ς</span></span>⟩ was also used word-medially at the end of a <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">compound</a> morpheme, e.g. "δυςκατανοήτων", marking the morpheme boundary between "δυς-κατανοήτων" ("difficult to understand"); modern standard practice is to spell "δυσκατανοήτων" with a non-final sigma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nick2004_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nick2004-28">[19]</a></sup> The letter sigma also has an alternative variant, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunate_sigma" title="Lunate sigma">lunate sigma</a> (uppercase <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">Ϲ</span></span>, lowercase <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ϲ</span></span>), which is used in all positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KellerRussell2012pp56_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-KellerRussell2012pp56-18">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mastronarde2013p12_27-7"><a href="#cite_note-Mastronarde2013p12-27">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson1912pp108144_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson1912pp108144-29">[20]</a></sup> This form of the letter developed during the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a> (323–31 BC) as a simplification of the older <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">Σ σ</span></span>/ς variant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson1912pp108144_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson1912pp108144-29">[20]</a></sup> Thus, the word <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><i lang="grc-Latn">stasis</i></span> can either be written <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">στάσις</span></span> or <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ϲτάϲιϲ</span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[21]</a></sup> In modern, edited Greek texts, the lunate sigma typically appears primarily in older typesetting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mastronarde2013p12_27-8"><a href="#cite_note-Mastronarde2013p12-27">[18]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-omegapronunciation-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-omegapronunciation_33-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The letter <a href="/wiki/Omega" title="Omega">omega</a> ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ω</span></span>⟩ is normally taught to English speakers as <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[oʊ]</span>, the long o as in English <i>g<u>o</u></i>, in order to more clearly distinguish it from omicron ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ο</span></span>⟩.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mastronarde2013p13_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mastronarde2013p13-32">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KellerRussell2012pp56_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-KellerRussell2012pp56-18">[15]</a></sup> This is not the sound it actually made in classical Attic Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mastronarde2013p13_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mastronarde2013p13-32">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KellerRussell2012pp56_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-KellerRussell2012pp56-18">[15]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<p>Among consonant letters, all letters that denoted voiced plosive consonants (<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/b, d, g/</span>) and aspirated plosives (<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/pʰ, tʰ, kʰ/</span>) in Ancient Greek stand for corresponding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fricative_consonant" title="Fricative consonant">fricative sounds</a> in Modern Greek. The correspondences are as follows:
</p>

</td>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_fricative" title="Voiceless velar fricative">x</a>]</span> ~ <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_palatal_fricative" title="Voiceless palatal fricative">ç</a>]</span>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Among the vowel symbols, Modern Greek sound values reflect the radical simplification of the vowel system of post-classical Greek, merging multiple formerly distinct vowel phonemes into a much smaller number. This leads to several groups of vowel letters denoting identical sounds today. Modern Greek orthography remains true to the historical spellings in most of these cases. As a consequence, the spellings of words in Modern Greek are often not predictable from the pronunciation alone, while the reverse mapping, from spelling to pronunciation, is usually regular and predictable.
</p><p>The following vowel letters and digraphs are involved in the mergers:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Modern Greek speakers typically use the same, modern symbol–sound mappings in reading Greek of all historical stages. In other countries, students of Ancient Greek may use a variety of <a href="/wiki/Pronunciation_of_Ancient_Greek_in_teaching" title="Pronunciation of Ancient Greek in teaching">conventional approximations</a> of the historical sound system in pronouncing Ancient Greek.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Digraphs_and_letter_combinations">Digraphs and letter combinations</span></h3>
<p>Several letter combinations have special conventional sound values different from those of their single components. Among them are several <a href="/wiki/Digraph_(orthography)" title="Digraph (orthography)">digraphs</a> of vowel letters that formerly represented <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthongs</a> but are now monophthongized. In addition to the four mentioned above (⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ει</span></span>, αι, οι, υι,⟩), there is also ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ηι, ωι</span></span>⟩, and ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ου</span></span>⟩, pronounced <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span>. The Ancient Greek diphthongs ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">αυ</span></span>⟩, ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ευ</span></span>⟩ and ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ηυ</span></span>⟩ are pronounced <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[av]</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ev]</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[iv]</span> in Modern Greek. In some environments, they are devoiced to <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[af]</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ef]</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[if]</span> respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[23]</a></sup> The Modern Greek consonant combinations ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">μπ</span></span>⟩ and ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ντ</span></span>⟩ stand for <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[b]</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[d]</span> (or <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[mb]</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[nd]</span>) respectively; ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">τζ</span></span>⟩ stands for <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[dz]</span> and ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">τσ</span></span>⟩ stands for <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[t͡s]</span>. In addition, both in Ancient and Modern Greek, the letter ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">γ</span></span>⟩, before another <a href="/wiki/Velar_consonant" title="Velar consonant">velar consonant</a>, stands for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Velar_nasal" title="Velar nasal">velar nasal</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ŋ]</span>; thus ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">γγ</span></span>⟩ and ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">γκ</span></span>⟩ are pronounced like English ⟨ng⟩. In analogy to ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">μπ</span></span>⟩ and ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ντ</span></span>⟩, ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">γκ</span></span>⟩ is also used to stand for <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[g]</span>. There are also the combinations ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">γχ</span></span>⟩ and ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">γξ</span></span>⟩.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diacritics">Diacritics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Greek_diacritics" title="Greek diacritics">Greek diacritics</a></div>
<p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polytonic_orthography" title="Polytonic orthography">polytonic orthography</a> traditionally used for ancient Greek, the stressed vowel of each word carries one of three accent marks: either the <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">acute accent</a> (<span style="font-size: 120%;"><span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ά</span></span></span>), the <a href="/wiki/Grave_accent" title="Grave accent">grave accent</a> (<span style="font-size: 120%;"><span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ὰ</span></span></span>), or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circumflex_accent" title="Circumflex accent">circumflex accent</a> (<span style="font-size: 120%;"><span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">α̃</span></span></span> or <span style="font-size: 120%;"><span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">α̑</span></span></span>). These signs were originally designed to mark different forms of the phonological <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" title="Pitch accent">pitch accent</a> in Ancient Greek. By the time their use became conventional and obligatory in Greek writing, in late antiquity, pitch accent was evolving into a single <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">stress accent</a>, and thus the three signs have not corresponded to a phonological distinction in actual speech ever since. In addition to the accent marks, every word-initial vowel must carry either of two so-called "breathing marks": the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiritus_asper" title="Spiritus asper">rough breathing</a> (<span style="font-size: 120%;"><span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἁ</span></span></span>), marking an <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/h/</span> sound at the beginning of a word, or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiritus_lenis" title="Spiritus lenis">smooth breathing</a> (<span style="font-size: 120%;"><span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἀ</span></span></span>), marking its absence. The letter rho (ρ), although not a vowel, also carries rough breathing in a word-initial position. If a rho was geminated within a word, the first <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ρ</span></span> always had the smooth breathing and the second the rough breathing (ῤῥ) leading to the transliteration rrh.
</p><p>The vowel letters ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">α, η, ω</span></span>⟩ carry an additional diacritic in certain words, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Iota_subscript" title="Iota subscript">iota subscript</a>, which has the shape of a small vertical stroke or a miniature ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ι</span></span>⟩ below the letter. This iota represents the former offglide of what were originally long diphthongs, ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ᾱι, ηι, ωι</span></span>⟩ (i.e. <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aːi, ɛːi, ɔːi/</span>), which became monophthongized during antiquity.
</p><p>Another diacritic used in Greek is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_dot_(diacritic)" title="Double dot (diacritic)">diaeresis</a> (<span style="font-size: 120%;"><span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">¨</span></span></span>), indicating a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hiatus_(linguistics)" title="Hiatus (linguistics)">hiatus</a>.
</p><p>This system of diacritics was first developed by the scholar <a href="/wiki/Aristophanes_of_Byzantium" title="Aristophanes of Byzantium">Aristophanes of Byzantium</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 257 – <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 185/180 BC), who worked at the <a href="/wiki/Musaeum" title="Musaeum">Musaeum</a> in Alexandria during the third century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDickey200792–93_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDickey200792–93-35">[24]</a></sup> Aristophanes of Byzantium also was the first to divide poems into lines, rather than writing them like prose, and also introduced a series of signs for <a href="/wiki/Textual_criticism" title="Textual criticism">textual criticism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDickey200793_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDickey200793-36">[25]</a></sup> In 1982, a new, simplified orthography, known as "monotonic", was adopted for official use in Modern Greek by the Greek state. It uses only a single accent mark, the acute (also known in this context as <i>tonos</i>, i.e. simply "accent"), marking the stressed syllable of polysyllabic words, and occasionally the diaeresis to distinguish diphthongal from digraph readings in pairs of vowel letters, making this monotonic system very similar to the accent mark system used in <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>. The polytonic system is still conventionally used for writing Ancient Greek, while in some book printing and generally in the usage of conservative writers it can still also be found in use for Modern Greek.
</p><p>Although it is not a diacritic, the <a href="/wiki/Comma" title="Comma">comma</a> has a similar function as a <a href="/wiki/Silent_letter" title="Silent letter">silent letter</a> in a handful of Greek words, principally distinguishing <span lang="el"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%8C,%CF%84%CE%B9#Greek" title="wikt:ό,τι">ό,τι</a></span> (<i>ó,ti</i>, "whatever") from <span lang="el"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%8C%CF%84%CE%B9#Greek" title="wikt:ότι">ότι</a></span> (<i>óti</i>, "that").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nicky_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-nicky-37">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Romanization">Romanization</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Greek" title="Romanization of Greek">Romanization of Greek</a></div>
<p>There are many different methods of rendering Greek text or Greek names in the Latin script.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999499–511_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999499–511-38">[27]</a></sup> The form in which classical Greek names are conventionally rendered in English goes back to the way Greek loanwords were incorporated into Latin in antiquity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999499–502_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999499–502-39">[28]</a></sup> In this system, ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">κ</span></span>⟩ is replaced with ⟨c⟩, the diphthongs ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">αι</span></span>⟩ and ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">οι</span></span>⟩ are rendered as ⟨ae⟩ and ⟨oe⟩ (or ⟨æ,œ⟩) respectively; and ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ει</span></span>⟩ and ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ου</span></span>⟩ are simplified to ⟨i⟩ and ⟨u⟩ respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999499–502,_510–511_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999499–502,_510–511-40">[29]</a></sup> Smooth breathing marks are usually ignored and rough breathing marks are usually rendered as the letter ⟨h⟩.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999499–502,_509_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999499–502,_509-41">[30]</a></sup> In modern scholarly transliteration of Ancient Greek, ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">κ</span></span>⟩ will usually be rendered as ⟨k⟩, and the vowel combinations ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">αι</span></span>, οι, ει, ου⟩ as ⟨ai, oi, ei, ou⟩ respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999499–511_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999499–511-38">[27]</a></sup> The letters ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">θ</span></span>⟩ and ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">φ</span></span>⟩ are generally rendered as ⟨th⟩ and ⟨ph⟩; ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">χ</span></span>⟩ as either ⟨ch⟩ or ⟨kh⟩; and word-initial ⟨<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ρ</span></span>⟩ as ⟨rh⟩.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999510–511_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999510–511-42">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Multiple different transcription conventions exist for Modern Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999505–507,_510–511_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999505–507,_510–511-43">[32]</a></sup> These differ widely, depending on their purpose, on how close they stay to the conventional letter correspondences of Ancient Greek-based transcription systems, and to what degree they attempt either an exact letter-by-letter <a href="/wiki/Transliteration" title="Transliteration">transliteration</a> or rather a phonetically-based transcription.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999505–507,_510–511_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999505–507,_510–511-43">[32]</a></sup> Standardized formal transcription systems have been defined by the <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">International Organization for Standardization</a> (as <a href="/wiki/ISO_843" title="ISO 843">ISO 843</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999505–507,_510–511_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerbrugghe1999505–507,_510–511-43">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISO843_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISO843-44">[33]</a></sup> by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Group_of_Experts_on_Geographical_Names" title="United Nations Group of Experts on Geographical Names">United Nations Group of Experts on Geographical Names</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[34]</a></sup> by the <a href="/wiki/ALA-LC_romanization" title="ALA-LC romanization">Library of Congress</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[35]</a></sup> and others.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Greek_alphabet" title="History of the Greek alphabet">History of the Greek alphabet</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dipylon_Inscription.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Dipylon_Inscription.JPG/220px-Dipylon_Inscription.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Dipylon_Inscription.JPG/330px-Dipylon_Inscription.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Dipylon_Inscription.JPG/440px-Dipylon_Inscription.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dipylon_Inscription.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dipylon_inscription" title="Dipylon inscription">Dipylon inscription</a>, one of the oldest known samples of the use of the Greek alphabet, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 740 BC</span></div></div></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean period</a>, from around the sixteenth century to the twelfth century BC, <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a> was used to write the earliest attested form of the Greek language, known as <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greek" title="Mycenaean Greek">Mycenaean Greek</a>. This writing system, unrelated to the Greek alphabet, last appeared in the thirteenth century BC. In the late ninth century BC or early eighth century BC, the Greek alphabet emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnston2003263–276_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnston2003263–276-2">[2]</a></sup> The period between the use of the two writing systems, during which no Greek texts are attested, is known as the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">Greek Dark Ages</a>. The Greeks adopted the alphabet from the earlier <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">Phoenician alphabet</a>, one of the closely related scripts used for the <a href="/wiki/West_Semitic_languages" title="West Semitic languages">West Semitic languages</a>, calling it Φοινικήια γράμματα 'Phoenician letters'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[36]</a></sup> However, the Phoenician alphabet is limited to consonants. When it was adopted for writing Greek, certain consonants were adapted to express vowels. The use of both vowels and consonants makes Greek the first <a href="/wiki/Alphabet" title="Alphabet">alphabet</a> in the narrow sense,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulmas1996_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulmas1996-6">[6]</a></sup> as distinguished from the <a href="/wiki/Abjad" title="Abjad">abjads</a> used in <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic languages</a>, which have letters only for consonants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDanielsBright19964_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDanielsBright19964-48">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NAMA_Alphabet_grec.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1398" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/NAMA_Alphabet_grec.jpg/220px-NAMA_Alphabet_grec.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/NAMA_Alphabet_grec.jpg/330px-NAMA_Alphabet_grec.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/NAMA_Alphabet_grec.jpg/440px-NAMA_Alphabet_grec.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NAMA_Alphabet_grec.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early Greek alphabet on pottery in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Archaeological_Museum_of_Athens" title="National Archaeological Museum of Athens">National Archaeological Museum of Athens</a></div></div></div>
<p>Greek initially took over all of the 22 letters of Phoenician. Five were reassigned to denote vowel sounds: the glide consonants <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j/</span> (<i><a href="/wiki/Yodh" title="Yodh">yodh</a></i>) and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/w/</span> (<i><a href="/wiki/Waw_(letter)" title="Waw (letter)">waw</a></i>) were used for [i] (Ι, <i><a href="/wiki/Iota" title="Iota">iota</a></i>) and [u] (Υ, <i><a href="/wiki/Upsilon" title="Upsilon">upsilon</a></i>) respectively; the <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">glottal stop</a> consonant <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʔ/</span> (<i><a href="/wiki/Aleph" title="Aleph">aleph</a></i>) was used for [a] (Α, <i><a href="/wiki/Alpha" title="Alpha">alpha</a></i>); the <a href="/wiki/Pharyngeal_consonant" title="Pharyngeal consonant">pharyngeal</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʕ/</span> (<i><a href="/wiki/Ayin" title="Ayin">ʿayin</a></i>) was turned into [o] (Ο, <i><a href="/wiki/Omicron" title="Omicron">omicron</a></i>); and the letter for <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/h/</span> (<i><a href="/wiki/He_(letter)" title="He (letter)">he</a></i>) was turned into [e] (Ε, <i><a href="/wiki/Epsilon" title="Epsilon">epsilon</a></i>). A doublet of waw was also borrowed as a consonant for [w] (Ϝ, <a href="/wiki/Digamma" title="Digamma">digamma</a>). In addition, the Phoenician letter for the emphatic glottal <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ħ/</span> (<i><a href="/wiki/Heth" title="Heth">heth</a></i>) was borrowed in two different functions by different dialects of Greek: as a letter for /h/ (Η, <a href="/wiki/Heta" title="Heta">heta</a>) by those dialects that had such a sound, and as an additional vowel letter for the long <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɛː/</span> (Η, <a href="/wiki/Eta" title="Eta">eta</a>) by those dialects that lacked the consonant. Eventually, a seventh vowel letter for the long <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɔː/</span> (Ω, <a href="/wiki/Omega" title="Omega">omega</a>) was introduced.
</p><p>Greek also introduced three new consonant letters for its aspirated plosive sounds and consonant clusters: Φ (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phi_(letter)" title="Phi (letter)">phi</a></i>) for <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/pʰ/</span>, Χ (<i><a href="/wiki/Chi_(letter)" title="Chi (letter)">chi</a></i>) for <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/kʰ/</span> and Ψ (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psi_(letter)" title="Psi (letter)">psi</a></i>) for <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ps/</span>. In western Greek variants, Χ was instead used for <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ks/</span> and Ψ for <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/kʰ/</span>. The origin of these letters is a matter of some debate.
</p>

<div style="clear:left;"></div>
</div>
<p>Three of the original Phoenician letters dropped out of use before the alphabet took its classical shape: the letter Ϻ (<i><a href="/wiki/San_(letter)" title="San (letter)">san</a></i>), which had been in competition with Σ (<i><a href="/wiki/Sigma" title="Sigma">sigma</a></i>) denoting the same phoneme /s/; the letter Ϙ (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qoppa" title="Qoppa">qoppa</a></i>), which was redundant with Κ (<i><a href="/wiki/Kappa" title="Kappa">kappa</a></i>) for /k/, and Ϝ (<i><a href="/wiki/Digamma" title="Digamma">digamma</a></i>), whose sound value /w/ dropped out of the spoken language before or during the classical period.
</p><p>Greek was originally written predominantly from right to left, just like Phoenician, but scribes could freely alternate between directions. For a time, a writing style with alternating right-to-left and left-to-right lines (called <i><a href="/wiki/Boustrophedon" title="Boustrophedon">boustrophedon</a></i>, literally "ox-turning", after the manner of an ox ploughing a field) was common, until in the classical period the left-to-right writing direction became the norm. Individual letter shapes were mirrored depending on the writing direction of the current line.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaic_variants">Archaic variants</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greek_alphabets" title="Archaic Greek alphabets">Archaic Greek alphabets</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Greek_epichoric_alphabets.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="776" data-file-width="921" decoding="async" height="337" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Ancient_Greek_epichoric_alphabets.svg/400px-Ancient_Greek_epichoric_alphabets.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Ancient_Greek_epichoric_alphabets.svg/600px-Ancient_Greek_epichoric_alphabets.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Ancient_Greek_epichoric_alphabets.svg/800px-Ancient_Greek_epichoric_alphabets.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Greek_epichoric_alphabets.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Distribution of "green", "red" and "blue" alphabet types, after Kirchhoff.</div></div></div>
<p>There were initially numerous <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greek_alphabets" title="Archaic Greek alphabets">local (epichoric) variants</a> of the Greek alphabet, which differed in the use and non-use of the additional vowel and consonant symbols and several other features. Epichoric alphabets are commonly divided into four major types according to their different treatments of additional consonant letters for the aspirated consonants (/pʰ, kʰ/) and consonant clusters (/ks, ps/) of Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVoutiras2007270_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVoutiras2007270-50">[38]</a></sup> These four types are often conventionally labelled as "green", "red", "light blue" and "dark blue" types, based on a colour-coded map in a seminal 19th-century work on the topic, <i>Studien zur Geschichte des griechischen Alphabets</i> by <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Kirchhoff" title="Adolf Kirchhoff">Adolf Kirchhoff</a> (1867).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVoutiras2007270_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVoutiras2007270-50">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The "green" (or southern) type is the most archaic and closest to the Phoenician.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodard201026–46_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodard201026–46-51">[39]</a></sup> The "red" (or western) type is the one that was later transmitted to the West and became the ancestor of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a>, and bears some crucial features characteristic of that later development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodard201026–46_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodard201026–46-51">[39]</a></sup> The "blue" (or eastern) type is the one from which the later standard Greek alphabet emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodard201026–46_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodard201026–46-51">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> used a local form of the "light blue" alphabet type until the end of the fifth century BC, which lacked the letters Ξ and Ψ as well as the vowel symbols Η and Ω.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodard201026–46_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodard201026–46-51">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJeffery196166_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJeffery196166-52">[40]</a></sup> In the Old Attic alphabet, <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ΧΣ</span></span> stood for <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ks/</span> and <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ΦΣ</span></span> for <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ps/</span>. <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Ε</span></span> was used for all three sounds <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e, eː, ɛː/</span> (correspondinɡ to classical <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Ε, ΕΙ, Η</span></span> respectively), and <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Ο</span></span> was used for all of <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/o, oː, ɔː/</span> (corresponding to classical <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Ο, ΟΥ, Ω</span></span> respectively).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJeffery196166_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJeffery196166-52">[40]</a></sup> The letter <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Η</span></span> (heta) was used for the consonant <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/h/</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJeffery196166_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJeffery196166-52">[40]</a></sup> Some variant local letter forms were also characteristic of Athenian writing, some of which were shared with the neighboring (but otherwise "red") alphabet of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euboia" title="Euboia">Euboia</a>: a form of <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Λ</span></span> that resembled a Latin <i>L</i> (<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek_Lambda_Athenian.svg"><img alt="Greek Lambda Athenian.svg" data-file-height="53" data-file-width="43" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Greek_Lambda_Athenian.svg/13px-Greek_Lambda_Athenian.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Greek_Lambda_Athenian.svg/19px-Greek_Lambda_Athenian.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Greek_Lambda_Athenian.svg/26px-Greek_Lambda_Athenian.svg.png 2x" width="13"/></a>) and a form of <span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%A3#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:Σ">Σ</a></span> that resembled a Latin <i>S</i> (<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek_Sigma_Z-shaped.svg"><img alt="Greek Sigma Z-shaped.svg" data-file-height="53" data-file-width="43" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Greek_Sigma_Z-shaped.svg/13px-Greek_Sigma_Z-shaped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Greek_Sigma_Z-shaped.svg/19px-Greek_Sigma_Z-shaped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Greek_Sigma_Z-shaped.svg/26px-Greek_Sigma_Z-shaped.svg.png 2x" width="13"/></a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJeffery196166_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJeffery196166-52">[40]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>*Upsilon is also derived from <a href="/wiki/Waw_(letter)" title="Waw (letter)">waw</a> (<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phoenician_waw.svg"><img alt="Phoenician waw.svg" data-file-height="85" data-file-width="85" decoding="async" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Phoenician_waw.svg/12px-Phoenician_waw.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Phoenician_waw.svg/18px-Phoenician_waw.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Phoenician_waw.svg/24px-Phoenician_waw.svg.png 2x" width="12"/></a>).
</p><p>The classical twenty-four-letter alphabet that is now used to represent the Greek language was originally the local alphabet of <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThreatte198026_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThreatte198026-53">[41]</a></sup> By the late fifth century BC, it was commonly used by many Athenians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThreatte198026_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThreatte198026-53">[41]</a></sup> In <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 403 BC, at the suggestion of the <a href="/wiki/Eponymous_archon" title="Eponymous archon">archon</a> <a href="/wiki/Eucleides" title="Eucleides">Eucleides</a>, the Athenian Assembly formally abandoned the Old Attic alphabet and adopted the Ionian alphabet as part of the democratic reforms after the <a href="/wiki/Phyle_Campaign" title="Phyle Campaign">overthrow</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Tyrants" title="Thirty Tyrants">Thirty Tyrants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThreatte198026_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThreatte198026-53">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorrocks2010xiix_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorrocks2010xiix-54">[42]</a></sup> Because of Eucleides's role in suggesting the idea to adopt the Ionian alphabet, the standard twenty-four-letter Greek alphabet is sometimes known as the "Eucleidean alphabet".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThreatte198026_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThreatte198026-53">[41]</a></sup> Roughly thirty years later, the Eucleidean alphabet was adopted in Boeotia and it may have been adopted a few years previously in <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPanayotou2007407_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPanayotou2007407-55">[43]</a></sup> By the end of the fourth century BC, it had displaced local alphabets across the Greek-speaking world to become the standard form of the Greek alphabet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPanayotou2007407_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPanayotou2007407-55">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Letter_names">Letter names</span></h3>
<p>When the Greeks adopted the Phoenician alphabet, they took over not only the letter shapes and sound values but also the names by which the sequence of the alphabet could be recited and memorized. In Phoenician, each letter name was a word that began with the sound represented by that letter; thus <i><a href="/wiki/Aleph" title="Aleph">ʾaleph</a></i>, the word for "ox", was used as the name for the glottal stop <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʔ/</span>, <i><a href="/wiki/Bet_(letter)" title="Bet (letter)">bet</a></i>, or "house", for the <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/b/</span> sound, and so on. When the letters were adopted by the Greeks, most of the Phoenician names were maintained or modified slightly to fit Greek phonology; thus, <i>ʾaleph, bet, gimel</i> became <i>alpha, beta, gamma</i>.
</p><p>The Greek names of the following letters are more or less straightforward continuations of their Phoenician antecedents. Between Ancient and Modern Greek, they have remained largely unchanged, except that their pronunciation has followed regular sound changes along with other words (for instance, in the name of <i>beta</i>, ancient /b/ regularly changed to modern /v/, and ancient /ɛː/ to modern /i/, resulting in the modern pronunciation <i>vita</i>). The name of lambda is attested in early sources as <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">λάβδα</span></span> besides <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">λάμβδα</span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KellerRussell2012p5_11-20"><a href="#cite_note-KellerRussell2012p5-11">[11]</a></sup> in Modern Greek the spelling is often <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">λάμδα</span></span>, reflecting pronunciation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KellerRussell2012p5_11-21"><a href="#cite_note-KellerRussell2012p5-11">[11]</a></sup> Similarly, iota is sometimes spelled <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">γιώτα</span></span> in Modern Greek (<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʝ]</span> is conventionally transcribed ⟨γ{ι,η,υ,ει,οι}⟩ word-initially and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intervocalic" title="Intervocalic">intervocalically</a> before <a href="/wiki/Back_vowel" title="Back vowel">back vowels</a> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span>). In the tables below, the Greek names of all letters are given in their traditional polytonic spelling; in modern practice, like with all other words, they are usually spelled in the simplified monotonic system.
</p>

<td style="text-align: center;">Α

<p>In the cases of the three historical sibilant letters below, the correspondence between Phoenician and Ancient Greek is less clear, with apparent mismatches both in letter names and sound values. The early history of these letters (and the fourth sibilant letter, obsolete <a href="/wiki/San_(letter)" title="San (letter)">san</a>) has been a matter of some debate. Here too, the changes in the pronunciation of the letter names between Ancient and Modern Greek are regular.
</p>

<td><span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">σίγμα</span></span></td>
<td><i>šin</i></td>
<td>siɡma
</td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[siɡma]</span></td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈsiɣma]</span></td>
<td><span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In the following group of consonant letters, the older forms of the names in Ancient Greek were spelled with <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">-εῖ</span></span>, indicating an original pronunciation with <i>-ē</i>. In Modern Greek these names are spelled with <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">-ι</span></span>.
</p>

<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[psi]</span></td>
<td><span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-psi.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e6/En-us-psi.ogg" title="En-us-psi.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The following group of vowel letters were originally called simply by their sound values as long vowels: ē, ō, ū, and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ɔ</span>. Their modern names contain adjectival qualifiers that were added during the Byzantine period, to distinguish between letters that had become confusable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KellerRussell2012p5_11-22"><a href="#cite_note-KellerRussell2012p5-11">[11]</a></sup> Thus, the letters ⟨ο⟩ and ⟨ω⟩, pronounced identically by this time, were called <i>o mikron</i> ("small o") and <i>o mega</i> ("big o") respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KellerRussell2012p5_11-23"><a href="#cite_note-KellerRussell2012p5-11">[11]</a></sup> The letter ⟨ε⟩ was called <i>e psilon</i> ("plain e") to distinguish it from the identically pronounced digraph ⟨αι⟩, while, similarly, ⟨υ⟩, which at this time was pronounced <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Close_front_rounded_vowel" title="Close front rounded vowel">y</a>]</span>, was called <i>y psilon</i> ("plain y") to distinguish it from the identically pronounced digraph ⟨οι⟩.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KellerRussell2012p5_11-24"><a href="#cite_note-KellerRussell2012p5-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>Some dialects of the Aegean and <a href="/wiki/Cypriot_Greek" title="Cypriot Greek">Cypriot</a> have retained <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_consonant" title="Long consonant">long consonants</a> and pronounce <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈɣamːa]</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈkapʰa]</span>; also, <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ήτα</span></span> has come to be pronounced <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈitʰa]</span> in Cypriot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Letter_shapes">Letter shapes</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gospel_Estienne_1550.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1276" data-file-width="812" decoding="async" height="471" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Gospel_Estienne_1550.jpg/300px-Gospel_Estienne_1550.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Gospel_Estienne_1550.jpg/450px-Gospel_Estienne_1550.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Gospel_Estienne_1550.jpg/600px-Gospel_Estienne_1550.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gospel_Estienne_1550.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 16th-century edition of the New Testament (<a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a>), printed in a renaissance typeface by <a href="/wiki/Claude_Garamond" title="Claude Garamond">Claude Garamond</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Theocritus-Syracusanus-et-al-Lodewijk-Caspar-Valckenaer_MG_0683_-_detail_-_Greek_text_-_Theocritus_-_Idyll_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="2642" decoding="async" height="41" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Theocritus-Syracusanus-et-al-Lodewijk-Caspar-Valckenaer_MG_0683_-_detail_-_Greek_text_-_Theocritus_-_Idyll_1.jpg/300px-Theocritus-Syracusanus-et-al-Lodewijk-Caspar-Valckenaer_MG_0683_-_detail_-_Greek_text_-_Theocritus_-_Idyll_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Theocritus-Syracusanus-et-al-Lodewijk-Caspar-Valckenaer_MG_0683_-_detail_-_Greek_text_-_Theocritus_-_Idyll_1.jpg/450px-Theocritus-Syracusanus-et-al-Lodewijk-Caspar-Valckenaer_MG_0683_-_detail_-_Greek_text_-_Theocritus_-_Idyll_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Theocritus-Syracusanus-et-al-Lodewijk-Caspar-Valckenaer_MG_0683_-_detail_-_Greek_text_-_Theocritus_-_Idyll_1.jpg/600px-Theocritus-Syracusanus-et-al-Lodewijk-Caspar-Valckenaer_MG_0683_-_detail_-_Greek_text_-_Theocritus_-_Idyll_1.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Theocritus-Syracusanus-et-al-Lodewijk-Caspar-Valckenaer_MG_0683_-_detail_-_Greek_text_-_Theocritus_-_Idyll_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Theocritus Idyll 1, lines 12–14, in script with abbreviations and ligatures from a caption in an illustrated edition of Theocritus. Lodewijk Caspar Valckenaer: <i>Carmina bucolica</i>, Leiden 1779.</div></div></div>
<p>Like Latin and other alphabetic scripts, Greek originally had only a single form of each letter, without a distinction between uppercase and lowercase. This distinction is an innovation of the modern era, drawing on different lines of development of the letter shapes in earlier handwriting.
</p><p>The oldest forms of the letters in antiquity are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majuscule" title="Majuscule">majuscule</a> forms. Besides the upright, straight inscriptional forms (capitals) found in stone carvings or incised pottery, more fluent writing styles adapted for handwriting on soft materials were also developed during antiquity. Such handwriting has been preserved especially from <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a> manuscripts in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> since the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a>. Ancient handwriting developed two distinct styles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uncial" title="Uncial">uncial</a> writing, with carefully drawn, rounded block letters of about equal size, used as a <a href="/wiki/Book_hand" title="Book hand">book hand</a> for carefully produced literary and religious manuscripts, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_cursive" title="Greek cursive">cursive</a> writing, used for everyday purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thompson_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-thompson-58">[46]</a></sup> The cursive forms approached the style of lowercase letter forms, with <a href="/wiki/Ascender_(typography)" title="Ascender (typography)">ascenders</a> and descenders, as well as many connecting lines and ligatures between letters.
</p><p>In the ninth and tenth century, uncial book hands were replaced with a new, more compact writing style, with letter forms partly adapted from the earlier cursive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thompson_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-thompson-58">[46]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Greek_minuscule" title="Greek minuscule">minuscule</a> style remained the dominant form of handwritten Greek into the modern era. During the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, western printers adopted the minuscule letter forms as lowercase printed typefaces, while modeling uppercase letters on the ancient inscriptional forms. The orthographic practice of using the letter case distinction for marking proper names, titles, etc. developed in parallel to the practice in Latin and other western languages.
</p>
