<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Derived_alphabets">Derived alphabets</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marsiliana_tablet.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="250" decoding="async" height="24" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Marsiliana_tablet.svg/300px-Marsiliana_tablet.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Marsiliana_tablet.svg/450px-Marsiliana_tablet.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Marsiliana_tablet.svg/600px-Marsiliana_tablet.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marsiliana_tablet.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The earliest Etruscan <a href="/wiki/Abecedarium" title="Abecedarium">abecedarium</a>, from Marsiliana d'Albegna, still almost identical with contemporaneous archaic Greek alphabets</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wulfila_bibel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="880" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Wulfila_bibel.jpg/220px-Wulfila_bibel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Wulfila_bibel.jpg/330px-Wulfila_bibel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Wulfila_bibel.jpg/440px-Wulfila_bibel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wulfila_bibel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Argenteus" title="Codex Argenteus">Codex Argenteus</a>, a 6th-century Bible manuscript in Gothic</div></div></div>
<p>The Greek alphabet was the model for various others:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulmas1996_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulmas1996-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_alphabet" title="Etruscan alphabet">Etruscan alphabet</a>;</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a>, together with various other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Italic_script" title="Old Italic script">ancient scripts in Italy</a>, adopted from an archaic form of the Greek alphabet brought to Italy by Greek colonists in the late 8th century BC, via <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_language" title="Etruscan language">Etruscan</a>;</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Gothic_alphabet" title="Gothic alphabet">Gothic alphabet</a>, devised in the 4th century AD to write the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic language</a>, based on a combination of Greek and Latin uncial models;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[47]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glagolitic_alphabet" title="Glagolitic alphabet">Glagolitic alphabet</a>, devised in the 9th century AD for writing <a href="/wiki/Old_Church_Slavonic" title="Old Church Slavonic">Old Church Slavonic</a>;</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">Cyrillic script</a>, which replaced the Glagolitic alphabet shortly afterwards.</li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coptic_Alphabet" title="Coptic Alphabet">Coptic Alphabet</a> used for writing the <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic language</a>.</li></ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Armenian_alphabet" title="Armenian alphabet">Armenian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georgian_alphabet" title="Georgian alphabet">Georgian</a> alphabets are almost certainly modeled on the Greek alphabet, but their graphic forms are quite different.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses">Other uses</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_for_other_languages">Use for other languages</span></h3>
<p>Apart from the daughter alphabets listed above, which were adapted from Greek but developed into separate writing systems, the Greek alphabet has also been adopted at various times and in various places to write other languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacrakis1996_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrakis1996-61">[49]</a></sup> For some of them, additional letters were introduced.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span></h4>
<ul><li>Most of the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alphabets_of_Asia_Minor" title="Alphabets of Asia Minor">alphabets of Asia Minor</a> were also adopted around the same time, as the early Greek alphabet was adopted from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoenician_Alphabet" title="Phoenician Alphabet">Phoenician Alphabet</a>. The Lydian and Carian alphabets are generally believed to derive from the Greek alphabet, although it is not clear which variant is the direct ancestor. While some of these alphabets such as <a href="/wiki/Phrygian_language" title="Phrygian language">Phrygian</a> had slight differences from the Greek counterpart, some like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carian_alphabet" title="Carian alphabet">Carian alphabet</a> had mostly different values and several other characters inherited from pre-Greek local scripts. They were in use c. 800–300 BC until all the <a href="/wiki/Anatolian_languages" title="Anatolian languages">Anatolian languages</a> were extinct due to <a href="/wiki/Hellenization" title="Hellenization">Hellenization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li>The original <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Italic_script" title="Old Italic script">Old Italic alphabets</a> was the early Greek alphabet with only slight modifications.</li>
<li>It was used in some <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Balkan_languages" title="Paleo-Balkan languages">Paleo-Balkan languages</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Thracian_language" title="Thracian language">Thracian</a>. For other neighboring languages or dialects, such as <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Macedonian_language" title="Ancient Macedonian language">Ancient Macedonian</a>, isolated words are preserved in Greek texts, but no continuous texts are preserved.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Greco-Iberian_alphabet" title="Greco-Iberian alphabet">Greco-Iberian alphabet</a> was used for writing the ancient <a href="/wiki/Iberian_language" title="Iberian language">Iberian language</a> in parts of modern Spain.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaulish_language" title="Gaulish language">Gaulish</a> inscriptions (in modern France) used the Greek alphabet until the Roman conquest</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic</a> text of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> was written in Greek letters in <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Hexapla" title="Hexapla">Hexapla</a>.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Bactrian_language" title="Bactrian language">Bactrian language</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranian language</a> spoken in what is now <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, was written in the Greek alphabet during the <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Empire</a> (65–250 AD). It adds an extra letter ⟨<a href="/wiki/Sho_(letter)" title="Sho (letter)">þ</a>⟩ for the <i>sh</i> sound <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiceless postalveolar fricative">ʃ</a>]</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESims-Williams1997_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESims-Williams1997-67">[55]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Coptic_alphabet" title="Coptic alphabet">Coptic alphabet</a> adds eight letters derived from <a href="/wiki/Demotic_(Egyptian)" title="Demotic (Egyptian)">Demotic</a>. It is still used today, mostly in Egypt, to write <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a>, the liturgical language of Egyptian Christians. Letters usually retain an <a href="/wiki/Uncial_script" title="Uncial script">uncial form</a> different from the forms used for Greek today. The alphabet of <a href="/wiki/Old_Nubian" title="Old Nubian">Old Nubian</a> is an adaptation of Coptic.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span></h4>
<ul><li>An 8th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> fragment preserves a text in the Greek alphabet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[56]</a></sup> as does a 9th or 10th century psalm translation fragment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[57]</a></sup></li>
<li>An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ossetic_language" title="Ossetic language">Old Ossetic</a> inscription of the 10th–12th centuries found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arxyz" title="Arxyz">Arxyz</a>, the oldest known attestation of an Ossetic language.</li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Nubian_language" title="Old Nubian language">Old Nubian language</a> of <a href="/wiki/Makuria" title="Makuria">Makuria</a> (modern Sudan) adds three Coptic letters, two letters derived from <a href="/wiki/Meroitic_script" title="Meroitic script">Meroitic script</a>, and a digraph of two Greek gammas used for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Velar_nasal" title="Velar nasal">velar nasal</a> sound.</li>
<li>Various <a href="/wiki/South_Slavic_languages" title="South Slavic languages">South Slavic</a> dialects, similar to the modern <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">Bulgarian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_language" title="Macedonian language">Macedonian languages</a>, have been written in Greek script.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiletich1920_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiletich1920-70">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMazonVaillant1938_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMazonVaillant1938-71">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKristophson197411_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKristophson197411-72">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeyfuss1989_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeyfuss1989-73">[61]</a></sup> The modern South Slavic languages now use modified <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_alphabets" title="Cyrillic alphabets">Cyrillic alphabets</a>.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern">Early modern</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karamanlidika_title_page_1784.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="559" decoding="async" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Karamanlidika_title_page_1784.JPG/220px-Karamanlidika_title_page_1784.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Karamanlidika_title_page_1784.JPG/330px-Karamanlidika_title_page_1784.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Karamanlidika_title_page_1784.JPG/440px-Karamanlidika_title_page_1784.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karamanlidika_title_page_1784.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>18th-century title page of a book printed in Karamanli Turkish</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a> spoken by <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox Christians</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Karamanlides" title="Karamanlides">Karamanlides</a></i>) was often written in Greek script, and called <i><a href="/wiki/Karamanli_Turkish" title="Karamanli Turkish">Karamanlidika</a></i>.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tosk_language" title="Tosk language">Tosk</a> <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a> was often written using the Greek alphabet, starting in about 1500.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElsie1991_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElsie1991-74">[62]</a></sup> The printing press at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moschopolis" title="Moschopolis">Moschopolis</a> published several Albanian texts in Greek script during the 18th century. It was only in 1908 that the <a href="/wiki/Bitola" title="Bitola">Monastir</a> conference standardized a <a href="/wiki/Albanian_alphabet" title="Albanian alphabet">Latin orthography</a> for both Tosk and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gheg_language" title="Gheg language">Gheg</a>. Greek spelling is still occasionally used for the local Albanian dialects (<a href="/wiki/Arvanitika" title="Arvanitika">Arvanitika</a>) in Greece.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gagauz_language" title="Gagauz language">Gagauz</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkic_language" title="Turkic language">Turkic language</a> of the northeast Balkans spoken by Orthodox Christians, was apparently written in Greek characters in the late 19th century.  In 1957, it was standardized on Cyrillic, and in 1996, a <a href="/wiki/Gagauz_alphabet" title="Gagauz alphabet">Gagauz alphabet</a> based on Latin characters was adopted (derived from the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_alphabet" title="Turkish alphabet">Turkish alphabet</a>).</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surguch" title="Surguch">Surguch</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkic_language" title="Turkic language">Turkic language</a>, was spoken by a small group of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox Christians</a> in northern Greece.  It is now written in Latin or Cyrillic characters.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Urum_language" title="Urum language">Urum</a> or Greek Tatar, spoken by Orthodox Christians, used the Greek alphabet.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judaeo-Spanish_language" title="Judaeo-Spanish language">Judaeo-Spanish</a> or Ladino, a Jewish dialect of Spanish, has occasionally been published in Greek characters in Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[63]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_mathematics_and_science">In mathematics and science</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Greek_letters_used_in_mathematics,_science,_and_engineering" title="Greek letters used in mathematics, science, and engineering">Greek letters used in mathematics, science, and engineering</a></div>
<p>Greek symbols are used as symbols in <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">sciences</a>. Many symbols have traditional uses, such as lower case epsilon (ε) for <a href="/wiki/Limit_(mathematics)" title="Limit (mathematics)">an arbitrarily small positive number</a>, lower case pi (π) for the <a href="/wiki/Pi" title="Pi">ratio of the circumference of a circle to its diameter</a>, capital sigma (Σ) for <a href="/wiki/Summation" title="Summation">summation</a>, and lower case sigma (σ) for <a href="/wiki/Standard_deviation" title="Standard deviation">standard deviation</a>. Formerly, the Greek letters were used for <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_naming#North_Atlantic_Ocean" title="Tropical cyclone naming">naming North Atlantic hurricanes</a> if the normal list ran out. This happened only in the <a href="/wiki/2005_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2005 Atlantic hurricane season">2005</a> and <a href="/wiki/2020_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2020 Atlantic hurricane season">2020</a> hurricane seasons for a total of 15 storms, the last one being <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Iota" title="Hurricane Iota">Hurricane Iota</a>. In May 2021 the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organisations</a> announced that the <a href="/wiki/Variants_of_SARS-CoV-2" title="Variants of SARS-CoV-2">variants of SARS-CoV-2</a> of the virus would be named using letters of the Greek alphabet to avoid stigma and simplify communications for non-scientific audiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">Astronomy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bayer_designation" title="Bayer designation">Bayer designation</a></div>
<p>Greek letters are used to denote the brighter stars within each of the eighty-eight <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellations</a>. In most constellations, the brightest star is designated Alpha and the next brightest Beta etc. For example, the brightest star in the constellation of <a href="/wiki/Centaurus" title="Centaurus">Centaurus</a> is known as <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri" title="Alpha Centauri">Alpha Centauri</a>. For historical reasons, the Greek designations of some constellations begin with a lower ranked letter.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_Phonetic_Alphabet">International Phonetic Alphabet</span></h3>
<p>Several Greek letters are used as phonetic symbols in the <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">International Phonetic Alphabet</a> (IPA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[66]</a></sup> Several of them denote fricative consonants; the rest stand for variants of vowel sounds. The glyph shapes used for these letters in specialized phonetic fonts is sometimes slightly different from the conventional shapes in Greek typography proper, with glyphs typically being more upright and using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serifs" title="Serifs">serifs</a>, to make them conform more with the typographical character of other, Latin-based letters in the phonetic alphabet. Nevertheless, in the Unicode encoding standard, the following three phonetic symbols are considered the same characters as the corresponding Greek letters proper:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>

<td><a href="/wiki/Voiced_bilabial_fricative" title="Voiced bilabial fricative">voiced bilabial fricative</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">θ</span></td>

</td></tr>

</p>

The IPA symbol for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palatal_lateral_approximant" title="Palatal lateral approximant">palatal lateral approximant</a> is ⟨<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ʎ</span>⟩, which looks similar to lambda, but is actually an inverted lowercase <i>y</i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_as_numerals">Use as numerals</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Greek_numerals" title="Greek numerals">Greek numerals</a></div>
<p>Greek letters were also used to write numbers. In the classical Ionian system, the first nine letters of the alphabet stood for the numbers from 1 to 9, the next nine letters stood for the multiples of 10, from 10 to 90, and the next nine letters stood for the multiples of 100, from 100 to 900. For this purpose, in addition to the 24 letters which by that time made up the standard alphabet, three otherwise obsolete letters were retained or revived: <a href="/wiki/Digamma" title="Digamma">digamma</a> ⟨Ϝ⟩ for 6, <a href="/wiki/Koppa_(letter)" title="Koppa (letter)">koppa</a> ⟨Ϙ⟩ for 90, and a rare Ionian letter for [ss], today called <a href="/wiki/Sampi" title="Sampi">sampi</a> ⟨Ͳ⟩, for 900. This system has remained in use in Greek up to the present day, although today it is only employed for limited purposes such as enumerating chapters in a book, similar to the way Roman numerals are used in English. The three extra symbols are today written as ⟨ϛ⟩, ⟨ϟ⟩ and ⟨ϡ⟩ respectively. To mark a letter as a numeral sign, a small stroke called <i><a href="/wiki/Greek_numerals#keraia" title="Greek numerals">keraia</a></i> is added to the right of it.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_by_student_fraternities_and_sororities">Use by student fraternities and sororities</span></h3>
<p>In North America, many college <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fraternities_and_sororities_in_North_America" title="Fraternities and sororities in North America">fraternities and sororities</a> are named with combinations of Greek letters, and are hence also known as "Greek letter organizations".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinterer2010377_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinterer2010377-80">[68]</a></sup> This naming tradition was initiated by the foundation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phi_Beta_Kappa_Society" title="Phi Beta Kappa Society">Phi Beta Kappa Society</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/College_of_William_and_Mary" title="College of William and Mary">College of William and Mary</a> in 1776.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinterer2010377_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinterer2010377-80">[68]</a></sup> The name of this fraternal organization is an acronym for the ancient Greek phrase Φιλοσοφία Βίου Κυβερνήτης (<i>Philosophia Biou Kybernētēs</i>), which means "Love of wisdom, the guide of life" and serves as the organization's motto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinterer2010377_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinterer2010377-80">[68]</a></sup> Sometimes early fraternal organizations were known by their Greek letter names because the mottos that these names stood for were secret and revealed only to members of the fraternity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinterer2010377_80-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinterer2010377-80">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Different chapters within the same fraternity are almost always (with a handful of exceptions) designated using Greek letters as serial numbers. The founding chapter of each respective organization is its A chapter. As an organization expands, it establishes a B chapter, a Γ chapter, and so on and so forth. In an organization that expands to more than 24 chapters, the chapter after Ω chapter is AA chapter, followed by AB chapter, etc. Each of these is still a "chapter Letter", albeit a double-digit letter just as 10 through 99 are double-digit numbers. The <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Roman alphabet</a> has a similar extended form with such double-digit letters when necessary, but it is used for columns in a table or chart rather than chapters of an organization.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Glyph_variants">Glyph variants</span></h2>
<p>Some letters can occur in variant shapes, mostly inherited from medieval <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Greek_alphabet#Later_developments" title="History of the Greek alphabet">minuscule</a> handwriting. While their use in normal typography of Greek is purely a matter of font styles, some such variants have been given separate encodings in <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a>.
</p>
<ul><li>The symbol ϐ ("curled beta") is a cursive variant form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta_(letter)" title="Beta (letter)">beta</a> (β). In the French tradition of Ancient Greek typography, β is used word-initially, and ϐ is used word-internally.</li>
<li>The letter <a href="/wiki/Delta_(letter)" title="Delta (letter)">delta</a> has a form resembling a cursive capital letter D; while not encoded as its own form, this form is included as part of the symbol for the <a href="/wiki/Greek_drachma" title="Greek drachma">drachma</a> (a Δρ digraph) in the <a href="/wiki/Currency_Symbols_(Unicode_block)" title="Currency Symbols (Unicode block)">Currency Symbols</a> block, at U+20AF (₯).</li>
<li>The letter <a href="/wiki/Epsilon" title="Epsilon">epsilon</a> can occur in two equally frequent stylistic variants, either shaped <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \epsilon \,\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>ϵ<!-- ϵ --></mi>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mspace width="negativethinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \epsilon \,\!}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\epsilon \,\!" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0908a81825c617639f3401ee1f29c4aae45b5c78" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; margin-right: -0.387ex; width:1.331ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> ('lunate epsilon', like a semicircle with a stroke) or <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \varepsilon \,\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>ε<!-- ε --></mi>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mspace width="negativethinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \varepsilon \,\!}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\varepsilon \,\!" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9b55ebcc38150a773c67f7a525004ed964e3efb7" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; margin-right: -0.387ex; width:1.471ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> (similar to a reversed number 3). The symbol ϵ (U+03F5) is designated specifically for the lunate form, used as a technical symbol.</li>
<li>The symbol ϑ ("script theta") is a cursive form of <a href="/wiki/Theta" title="Theta">theta</a> (θ), frequent in handwriting, and used with a specialized meaning as a technical symbol.</li>
<li>The symbol ϰ ("kappa symbol") is a cursive form of <a href="/wiki/Kappa" title="Kappa">kappa</a> (κ), used as a technical symbol.</li>
<li>The symbol ϖ ("variant pi") is an archaic script form of <a href="/wiki/Pi" title="Pi">pi</a> (π), also used as a technical symbol.</li>
<li>The letter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rho_(letter)" title="Rho (letter)">rho</a> (ρ) can occur in different stylistic variants, with the descending tail either going straight down or curled to the right. The symbol ϱ (U+03F1) is designated specifically for the curled form, used as a technical symbol.</li>
<li>The letter <a href="/wiki/Sigma" title="Sigma">sigma</a>, in standard orthography, has two variants: ς, used only at the ends of words, and σ, used elsewhere. The form ϲ ("<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunate_sigma" title="Lunate sigma">lunate sigma</a>", resembling a Latin <i><a href="/wiki/C" title="C">c</a></i>) is a medieval stylistic variant that can be used in both environments without the final/non-final distinction.</li>
<li>The capital letter <a href="/wiki/Upsilon" title="Upsilon">upsilon</a> (Υ) can occur in different stylistic variants, with the upper strokes either straight like a Latin <i>Y</i>, or slightly curled. The symbol ϒ (U+03D2) is designated specifically for the curled form (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Upsilon }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi mathvariant="normal">Υ<!-- Υ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \Upsilon }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\Upsilon " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/96e8c0694e5270ebf60de3a794a27f94a063020a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.808ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>), used as a technical symbol, e.g. in physics.</li>
<li>The letter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phi_(letter)" title="Phi (letter)">phi</a> can occur in two equally frequent stylistic variants, either shaped as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \textstyle \phi \,\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>ϕ<!-- ϕ --></mi>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mspace width="negativethinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \textstyle \phi \,\!}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\textstyle \phi \,\!" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cfa509d51e0009b72b664622e0fca67d9fb84ad2" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-right: -0.387ex; width:1.773ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> (a circle with a vertical stroke through it) or as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \textstyle \varphi \,\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>φ<!-- φ --></mi>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mspace width="negativethinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \textstyle \varphi \,\!}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\textstyle \varphi \,\!" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d41fb456a56c324671203adee6b5238fed8782a0" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; margin-right: -0.387ex; width:1.907ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> (a curled shape open at the top). The symbol ϕ (U+03D5) is designated specifically for the closed form, used as a technical symbol.</li>
<li>The letter <a href="/wiki/Omega" title="Omega">omega</a> has at least three stylistic variants of its capital form. The standard is the "open omega" (Ω), resembling an open partial circle with the opening downward and the ends curled outward. The two other stylistic variants are seen more often in modern typography, resembling a raised and underscored circle (roughly <sup>o̲</sup>), where the underscore may or may not be touching the circle on a tangent (in the former case it resembles a superscript omicron similar to that found in the <a href="/wiki/Numero_sign" title="Numero sign">numero sign</a> or masculine <a href="/wiki/Ordinal_indicator" title="Ordinal indicator">ordinal indicator</a>; in the latter, it closely resembles some forms of the Latin letter Q). The open omega is always used in symbolic settings and is encoded in <a href="/wiki/Letterlike_Symbols" title="Letterlike Symbols">Letterlike Symbols</a> (U+2126) as a separate code point for backward compatibility.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Computer_encodings">Computer encodings</span></h2>
<p>For computer usage, a variety of encodings have been used for Greek online, many of them documented in <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1067248974">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit;word-wrap:break-word}.mw-parser-output .citation q{quotes:"\"""\"""'""'"}.mw-parser-output .citation:target{background-color:rgba(0,127,255,0.133)}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-free a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-free a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/65/Lock-green.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .id-lock-registration a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-registration a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d6/Lock-gray-alt-2.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-subscription a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-subscription a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/aa/Lock-red-alt-2.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .cs1-ws-icon a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg")right 0.1em center/12px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .cs1-code{color:inherit;background:inherit;border:none;padding:inherit}.mw-parser-output .cs1-hidden-error{display:none;color:#d33}.mw-parser-output .cs1-visible-error{color:#d33}.mw-parser-output .cs1-maint{display:none;color:#3a3;margin-left:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-format{font-size:95%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-left{padding-left:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-right{padding-right:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .citation .mw-selflink{font-weight:inherit}</style><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RFC_(identifier)" title="RFC (identifier)">RFC</a> <a class="external text" href="https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc1947" rel="nofollow">1947</a>.
</p><p>The two principal ones still used today are <a href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_8859-7" title="ISO/IEC 8859-7">ISO/IEC 8859-7</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a>. ISO 8859-7 supports only the monotonic orthography; Unicode supports both the monotonic and polytonic orthographies.
</p>
<h3><span id="ISO.2FIEC_8859-7"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="ISO/IEC_8859-7">ISO/IEC 8859-7</span></h3>
<p>For the range A0–FF (hex), it follows the Unicode range 370–3CF (see below) except that some symbols, like ©, ½, § etc. are used where Unicode has unused locations. Like all ISO-8859 encodings, it is equal to ASCII for 00–7F (hex).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_in_Unicode">Greek in Unicode</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Greek_script_in_Unicode" title="Greek script in Unicode">Greek script in Unicode</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_and_Coptic" title="Greek and Coptic">Greek and Coptic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Greek_Extended" title="Greek Extended">Greek Extended</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> supports <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polytonic_orthography" title="Polytonic orthography">polytonic orthography</a> well enough for ordinary continuous text in modern and ancient Greek, and even many archaic forms for <a href="/wiki/Epigraphy" title="Epigraphy">epigraphy</a>. With the use of <a href="/wiki/Combining_character" title="Combining character">combining characters</a>, Unicode also supports Greek <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dialectology" title="Dialectology">dialectology</a> and various other specialized requirements. Most current text rendering engines do not render diacritics well, so, though alpha with <a href="/wiki/Macron_(diacritic)" title="Macron (diacritic)">macron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">acute</a> can be <i>represented</i> as U+03B1 U+0304 U+0301, this rarely renders well: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ᾱ́</span></span>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>There are two main blocks of Greek characters in <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a>. The first is "Greek and Coptic" (U+0370 to U+03FF). This block is based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISO_8859-7" title="ISO 8859-7">ISO 8859-7</a> and is sufficient to write Modern Greek. There are also some archaic letters and Greek-based technical symbols.
</p><p>This block also supports the <a href="/wiki/Coptic_alphabet" title="Coptic alphabet">Coptic alphabet</a>. Formerly, most Coptic letters shared codepoints with similar-looking Greek letters; but in many scholarly works, both scripts occur, with quite different letter shapes, so as of Unicode 4.1, Coptic and Greek were disunified. Those Coptic letters with no Greek equivalents still remain in this block (U+03E2 to U+03EF).
</p><p>To write polytonic Greek, one may use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combining_diacritical_mark" title="Combining diacritical mark">combining diacritical marks</a> or the precomposed characters in the "Greek Extended" block (U+1F00 to U+1FFF).
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Encodings_with_a_subset_of_the_Greek_alphabet">Encodings with a subset of the Greek alphabet</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> code pages <a href="/wiki/Code_page_437" title="Code page 437">437</a>, <a href="/wiki/Code_page_860" title="Code page 860">860</a>, <a href="/wiki/Code_page_861" title="Code page 861">861</a>, <a href="/wiki/Code_page_862" title="Code page 862">862</a>, <a href="/wiki/Code_page_863" title="Code page 863">863</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Code_page_865" title="Code page 865">865</a> contain the letters ΓΘΣΦΩαδεπστφ (plus β as an alternative interpretation for <a href="/wiki/%C3%9F" title="ß">ß</a>).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-longepsilon-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-longepsilon_49-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-longepsilon_49-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Epsilon ⟨ε⟩ and omicron ⟨ο⟩ originally could denote both short and long vowels in pre-classical archaic Greek spelling, just like other vowel letters. They were restricted to the function of short vowel signs in classical Greek, as the long vowels <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Close-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close-mid front unrounded vowel">eː</a>/</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Close-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close-mid back rounded vowel">oː</a>/</span> came to be spelled instead with the digraphs ⟨ει⟩ and ⟨ου⟩, having phonologically merged with a corresponding pair of former diphthongs /ei/ and /ou/ respectively.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width" style="column-width: 30em;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTESwiggers1996-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwiggers1996_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFSwiggers1996">Swiggers 1996</a>.</span>
</li>

Karageorghis, eds, <i>Sea Routes from Sidon to Huelva: Interconnections in the Mediterranean</i> 2003:263-76, summarizes the present scholarship on the dating.</span>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTECook19879-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTECook19879_4-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFCook1987">Cook 1987</a>, p. 9.</span>

<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.arcalog.com/wp-content/uploads/2014/04/Near-Eastern-Chronology-and-the-development-of-the-Greek-Alphabet.pdf" rel="nofollow">The Development of the Greek Alphabet within the Chronology of the ANE</a> (2009), Quote: "Naveh gives four major reasons why it is universally agreed that the Greek alphabet was developed from an early Phoenician alphabet.<br/>1 According to Herodutous "the Phoenicians who came with Cadmus... brought into Hellas the alphabet, which had hitherto been unknown, as I think, to the Greeks."<br/>2 The Greek Letters, alpha, beta, gimmel have no meaning in Greek but the meaning of most of their Semitic equivalents is known. For example, 'aleph' means 'ox', 'bet' means 'house' and 'gimmel' means 'throw stick'.<br/>3 Early Greek letters are very similar and sometimes identical to the West Semitic letters.<br/>4 The letter sequence between the Semitic and Greek alphabets is identical. (Naveh 1982)"</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-thompson-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-thompson_58-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-thompson_58-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFThompson1912">Thompson 1912</a>, pp. 102–103</span>

<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMurdoch2004">Murdoch 2004</a>, p. 156</span>

<li id="cite_note-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-60">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">George L. Campbell, Christopher Moseley, <i>The Routledge Handbook of Scripts and Alphabets</i>, pp. 51<i>ff</i>, 96<i>ff</i></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrakis1996-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacrakis1996_61-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMacrakis1996">Macrakis 1996</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="https://www.repository.cam.ac.uk/bitstream/handle/1810/305290/Understanding%20Relations%20Between%20Scripts%20II_PrintPDF_1.pdf" rel="nofollow"><b>Understanding Relations Between Scripts II</b></a> by <b>Philip J Boyes</b> &amp; <b>Philippa M Steele</b>. <i>Published in the UK in 2020 by Oxbow Books</i>: "The Carian alphabet resembles the Greek alphabet, though, as in the case of Phrygian, no single Greek variant can be identified as its ancestor", "It is generally assumed that the Lydian alphabet is derived from the Greek alphabet, but the exact relationship remains unclear (Melchert 2004)"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Lycian-alphabet" rel="nofollow"><b>Britannica - Lycian Alphabet</b></a> "The Lycian alphabet is clearly related to the Greek, but the exact nature of the relationship is uncertain. Several letters appear to be related to symbols of the Cretan and Cyprian writing systems."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="https://scriptsource.org/cms/scripts/page.php?item_id=script_detail&amp;key=Cari" rel="nofollow"><b>Scriptsource.org - Carian</b></a>"Visually, the letters bear a close resemblance to Greek letters. Decipherment was initially attempted on the assumption that those letters which looked like Greek represented the same sounds as their closest visual Greek equivalents. However it has since been established that the phonetic values of the two scripts are very different. For example the theta θ symbol represents ‘th’ in Greek but ‘q’ in Carian. Carian was generally written from left to right, although Egyptian writers wrote primarily from right to left. It was written without spaces between words."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="https://omniglot.com/writing/carian.php" rel="nofollow"><b>Omniglot.com - Carian</b></a> "The Carian alphabet appears in about 100 pieces of graffiti inscriptions left by Carian mercenaries who served in Egypt. A number of clay tablets, coins and monumental inscriptions have also been found. It was possibly derived from the Phoenician alphabet."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-66">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="https://www.degruyter.com/document/doi/10.31826/jlr-2019-163-405/pdf" rel="nofollow"><b>Ancient Anatolian languages and cultures in contact: some methodological observations</b></a> by <b>Paola Cotticelli-Kurras</b> &amp; <b>Federico Giusfredi</b> <i>(University of Verona, Italy)</i> "During the Iron ages, with a brand new political balance and cultural scenario, the cultures and languages of Anatolia maintained their position of a bridge between the Aegean and the Syro-Mesopotamian worlds, while the North-West Semitic cultures of the Phoenicians and of the Aramaeans also entered the scene. Assuming the 4th century and the <b>hellenization of Anatolia</b> as the <b>terminus ante quem</b>, the correct perspective of a contact-oriented study of the Ancient Anatolian world needs to take as an object a large net of cultures that evolved and changed over almost 16 centuries of documentary history."</span>
</li>
